    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="3113bc5f36764192b6e0445af5894a5a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '2bec8878-c542-43ef-ac18-ea20191881c1'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d9436784" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Single atom Cu regulated Bi@C with Cu–C<sub>3</sub> sites have been innovatively designed by the simple pyrolysis of metal-organic framework. The Cu–C<sub>3</sub> sites can serve as both a modulator to catalyze the formation of inorganic-rich and powerful solid electrolyte interphase and a promoter to facilitate the adsorption and diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>, which endow the sample with high-efficient sodium ion storage performance.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1ff86252-0d0d-4d88-970d-73e06ef90133/anie202417602-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1ff86252-0d0d-4d88-970d-73e06ef90133/anie202417602-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4219dd35-95ea-49c5-9c6a-53aae342982c/anie202417602-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1ff86252-0d0d-4d88-970d-73e06ef90133/anie202417602-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d9436787" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Regulating interfacial chemistry at electrode-electrolyte interface by designing catalytic electrode material is crucial and challenging for optimizing battery performance. Herein, a novel single atom Cu regulated Bi@C with Cu−C<sub>3</sub> site (Bi@SA Cu−C) have been designed via the simple pyrolysis of metal-organic framework. Experimental investigations and theoretical calculations indicate the Cu−C<sub>3</sub> sites accelerate the dissociation of P−F and C−O bonds in NaPF<sub>6</sub>-ether-based electrolyte and catalyze the formation of inorganic-rich and powerful solid electrolyte interphase. In addition, the Cu−C<sub>3</sub> sites with delocalized electron around Cu trigger an uneven charge distribution and induce an in-plane local electric field, which facilitates the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> and reduces the Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barrier. Consequently, the obtained Bi@SA Cu−C achieves a state-of-the-art reversible capacity, ultrahigh rate capability, and long-term cycling durability. The as-constructed full cell delivers a high capacity of 351 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to an energy density of 265 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides a new strategy to realize high-efficient sodium ion storage for alloy-based anode through constructing single-atom modulator integrated catalysis and promotion effect into one entity.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417602-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417602-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the deepening of the globalization layout of the new energy industry, the status of rechargeable batteries in the field of energy storage has reached a whole new level.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0001_R_d9436776e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0002_R_d9436776e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention due to the similar properties of sodium to lithium, abundant sodium reserves, and cost-effective, which endow it as one of the most competitive candidate for large scale energy storage systems.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0003_R_d9436776e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a>-<a href="#anie202417602-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0005_R_d9436776e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Nano">5</a></sup></span> In the view of SIBs system, the design of suitable anode materials is one of the major challenge in determining the performance of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0006_R_d9436776e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett.">6</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0007_R_d9436776e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a></sup></span> As a representative alloy based materials, bismuth (Bi) has the merits of high electronic/ionic conductivity, suitable redox reaction potential, and resource-richness and eco-friendliness. Nevertheless, the large volume change (≈244 %) and serious aggregation of Bi particles during the sodiation/desodiation process usually cause the pulverization of electrode and the separation of active materials from the collector, leading to unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), thus poor capacity retention and rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0008_R_d9436776e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#anie202417602-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0010_R_d9436776e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span> The electrochemical performance comprehensively relies on the intrinsic characteristic of electrode, the solvation structure of electrolyte, and the interfacial chemistry of electrode and electrolyte. To address the bottleneck, regulating SEI layer is considered to be an effective method to realize the long-term cycle and high rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0011_R_d9436776e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span> The SEI provides a passivation layer on the anode surface, which enable the ion penetrate into the anode and prevent the continuous reduction via the electronic insulating properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0012_R_d9436776e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Joule">12</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0013_R_d9436776e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Energy">13</a></sup></span> An ideal SEI film usually presents homogeneous and stable characteristics and consists of inorganic-rich components.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0014_R_d9436776e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0015_R_d9436776e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span> Electrochemical reduction of electrolyte to form SEI layer at the interface of electrode and electrolyte is not only affected by electrolyte component, but also governed by the surface property of electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0016_R_d9436776e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a>-<a href="#anie202417602-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0018_R_d9436776e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span> Thus, it is essential to engineer an advanced surface configuration to achieve a powerful and stable SEI layers.</p>
         
         <p>In recent years, metal-single atom (MSA) have been extensively investigated in electrocatalytic fields due to their clear active site, 100 % atomic utilization and adjustable electronic properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0019_R_d9436776e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Electrochem. Energy R.">19</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0020_R_d9436776e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Rev.">20</a></sup></span> The excellent catalytic ability has inspired researchers to explore its application on advanced battery systems, such as lithium-sulfur batteries,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0021_R_d9436776e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> sodium-ion hybrid capacitors<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0022_R_d9436776e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span> and sodium-ion battery.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0023_R_d9436776e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span> Some pioneer studies have demonstrated that MSA can boost the affinity and reduce the migration resistance of alkali metal ions, as well as produce abundant delocalized electrons for improving electron conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0024_R_d9436776e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Matter">24</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0025_R_d9436776e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span> More importantly, MSA can also modulate the decomposition process of the electrolyte, which creates a favorable condition for forming stable and robust SEI films.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0026_R_d9436776e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a></sup></span> For the MSA, the local microenvironment can serve as the electrons and/or ion acceptor, which have a significant effect on the adsorption and activation of Na<sup>+</sup>. Hence, the reasonable design of coordination configuration of MSA, such as the coordination number and type, enable the MSA possess extensive possibilities in adjusting the reaction pathway and promoting the targeted catalytic processes. Typically, the electronic structure of active sites is modulated by introducing different heteroatoms into the carbon framework to form M−X−C (where X represents the N, O, P, and S element), thereby optimizing its catalytic performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0027_R_d9436776e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a>-<a href="#anie202417602-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0029_R_d9436776e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span> For example, Lu et al.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0018_R_d9436776e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span> demonstrated Zn doping hard carbon with a Zn−N<sub>4</sub> coordination can enhance Na<sup>+</sup> transportation and catalyze electrolyte decomposition to regulate the SEI layer. Qiu et al.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0028_R_d9436776e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span> reported Ni-single atoms anchored N, P co-doped hard carbon, which exhibit a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), excellent rate capability, and long cycling stability. Zhou et al. stabilized Cu single atoms by P moiety to build a Cu−P<sub>3</sub> configuration, which enhanced the electron transfer and enable Na to maintain an ionic state during discharging process. To the best of our knowledge, most single-atom reported in batteries exhibit the coordination structure of M−X−C in the first coordination sphere. And only a few frontier studies have focused on the direct coordination of metal atoms with carbon in the field of electrocatalysis since the better electronegative heteroatoms are more likely to coordinate with metal atoms.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0030_R_d9436776e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">30</a>-<a href="#anie202417602-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0035_R_d9436776e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a></sup></span> Therefore, it is great meaningful to construct the direct coordination of metal with C to fill the research gap and investigate the relationship between the structure and battery performance by a simple and convenient method.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, single atomic Cu regulated Bi@C with a Cu−C<sub>3</sub> sites (Bi@SA Cu−C) were proposed innovatively using metal-organic framework (MOF) as the precursor followed by a facile and effective pyrolysis strategy. The effects of C−C<sub>3</sub> sites on the interfacial chemistry and sodium storage performance were elaborately studied and illuminated by density functional theory (DFT) calculation, and in-situ and ex-situ experimental characteristic. Attractively, The Cu−C<sub>3</sub> sites can act as a tandem effect to reduce the dissociation energy of P−F and C−O bond in NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME (1,2-Dimethoxyethane) electrolyte to form a thin and inorganic-rich SEI film and lower the diffusion barrier to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> migration. As a results, the obtained Bi@SA Cu−C material achieves a state-of-the-art reversible capacity of 404 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, ultrahigh rate capability (315 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 70 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and long-term cycle stability. The as constructed full-cell delivers a high capacity of 351 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to an energy-density of 265 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417602-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417602-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Bi@SA Cu−C was innovatively developed via a two-step process, i.e. the synthesis of Cu/Bi-MOF precursor and subsequent thermal treatment process, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0001">1a</a>. Firstly, the Bi/Cu−MOF precursor with Cu doping was synthesized utilizing the versatile solvothermal method. From scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images, all samples present similar microrod-shaped morphology with smooth surfaces (Figure S1). With the Cu doping into the Bi-MOF, two additional peaks associated with the Cu−MOF appear, indicating the synthesis of Bi/Cu−MOF. The Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) analysis confirm Cu<sup>2+</sup> ions successfully substituted for part of the Bi<sup>3+</sup> ions and coordinated with the carboxyl group (Figure S2). During the thermal treatment process in inert atmosphere, the structural evolution of MOF template is predominantly governed by the Kirkendall effect, which allows the in situ formation of metal/carbon composite owing to the faster outward diffusion of metal ions relative to the inward diffusion of amorphous oxygen.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0036_R_d9436776e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0037_R_d9436776e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span> Thus, during the subsequent pyrolysis of Bi/Cu−MOF at 700 °C in N<sub>2</sub>, the organic ligand completely carbonizes, and Bi<sup>3+</sup> diffuse outward to form metallic Bi nanoparticles (Figure S3). Meanwhile, the Cu ions are incorporated into the carbon framework, forming a new coordination configuration with the C atom. Consequently, the Bi nanoparticles coated by Cu single-atoms regulated carbon framework were successfully obtained. Notably, with increasing the amount of Cu doping, the Cu single-atoms gather together to form the metallic Cu nanoparticles, and the corresponding sample is designated as Bi/Cu@C. As displayed in Figure 1b and Figure S4, the Bi@SA Cu−C effectively inherits the morphology of the precursor and exhibits homogeneous and monodispersed microrod architecture with an average diameter of 2 μm. Compared with the parental Bi/Cu−MOF, the surface of Bi@SA Cu−C become rough owing to the decomposition of organic ligand and the fast outward diffusion of metal ions. The interior and exterior of Bi@SA Cu−C material comprises numerous homogeneous Bi pellets embedded in the carbon framework, creating a relatively rough surface (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0001">1c</a>). The carbon-coated Bi pellets could enhance the specific surface area and provide additional binding sites for sodium ion reactions. Moreover, the obvious carbon framework can improve conductivity, promote electron transfer, and limit the aggregation of the Bi pellets, thus improving the cycling stability and rate capability. The layer spacing of 0.329 nm in HRTEM image correspond to the (102) planes of Bi (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0001">1d</a>). Spherical aberration-corrected high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (AC HAADF-STEM) undoubtedly confirms the atomically dispersed Cu species (bright dots marked by red circles) in carbon framework (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0001">1e</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0001">f</a>) owing to the significant difference in atomic number between C and Cu. Moreover, the high reliability of the single atom structure for Bi@SA Cu−C was further confirmed by the image in different regions (Figure S5). In atomic resolution HAADF image, an interlayer spacing of 0.186 nm is indexed to the (202) crystal plane of metal Bi (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0001">1g</a>). The elemental mappings indicate the uniform distribution of Bi, Cu, and C elements without obvious aggregations (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0001">1h</a>). The atomic amount of Cu is determined to be 1.41 wt % according to inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417602-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f162300c-8118-47fd-88d5-a59b62469236/anie202417602-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f162300c-8118-47fd-88d5-a59b62469236/anie202417602-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/16a4666f-e1e7-42ec-9ca1-dbf89a60ce33/anie202417602-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f162300c-8118-47fd-88d5-a59b62469236/anie202417602-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417602-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417602" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of Bi@SA Cu−C. b) SEM, c,d) (HR)TEM, e–g) AC HAADF-STEM images, and h) Elemental mappings of Bi@SA Cu−C.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2a</a> present the X-ray diffraction (XRD) Rietveld refinement results of Bi@SA Cu−C. All diffraction peaks can be defined as a hexagonal phase Bi (JCPDS card no. 851329) with a reliable weighting factor (R<sub>wp</sub>=9.02 %). No significant diffraction peaks of Cu species are observed in the XRD patterns, which indicates the negligible effect after trace amount of Cu doping and also provides an important evidence for the existence of Cu single atom.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0029_R_d9436776e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span> With increasing the amount of Cu doping, the diffraction peak of metallic Cu appears in the XRD spectrum for Bi/Cu@C sample (Figure S6). XPS analysis were performed to understand the electronic state and composition of the samples (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2d</a> and Figure S7). For the C1s spectrum of Bi@C, there exist three peaks at 284.8 eV, 285.6 eV, and 288.3 eV, belonged to C−C, C−O, and C=O, respectively. While the additional peak at 283.5 eV for Bi@SA Cu−C is successfully indexed to C−Cu (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0038_R_d9436776e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span> In Bi 4 f spectrum, the peaks at 164.4 and 159.2 eV is assigned to Bi 4f<sub>5/2</sub> and Bi 4f<sub>7/2</sub>, respectively, and those at 162.48 eV and 157.18 eV can be deconvolved to Bi<sup>0</sup> (Figure S7b).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0039_R_d9436776e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Storage Mater.">39</a></sup></span> The peaks at 532.2 and 530.2 eV in O1s spectra result from the Bi−O and C−O, respectively, owing to the oxidation of Bi on the surface during sample preparation (Figure S7c). At the same time, the peaks centered at 952.9 and 933.0 eV for Cu 2p in Bi@SA Cu−C are also detected, which can be associated with Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> of Cu<sup>δ+</sup> (1&lt;δ&lt;2) species (Figure S7d).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0031_R_d9436776e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0040_R_d9436776e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">40</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417602-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/052cbb1d-bad7-4b25-9a74-af63df5fc838/anie202417602-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/052cbb1d-bad7-4b25-9a74-af63df5fc838/anie202417602-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9e4e91bb-19a2-45e8-aeb6-1a2386817bec/anie202417602-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/052cbb1d-bad7-4b25-9a74-af63df5fc838/anie202417602-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417602-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417602" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) XRD Rietveld refinement result of Bi@Cu−C. b) Cu K-edge XANES spectra of Bi@SA Cu−C, Cu foil, Cu<sub>2</sub>O, and CuO. c) Corresponding Cu K-edge first derivative plot. d) XPS spectra for C 1s of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C. e) FT k<sup>3</sup>-weighted EXAFS spectra for the Cu K-edge of Bi@SA Cu−C, Cu foil, Cu<sub>2</sub>O, and CuO. f) k space EXAFS curves of Bi@SA Cu−C, Cu foil, Cu<sub>2</sub>O and CuO. g-h) Wavelet-transformed k<sup>3</sup>-weighted EXAFS spectra of (g) Bi@SA Cu−C, (h) Cu foil, (i) Cu<sub>2</sub>O and (j) CuO.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) were conducted to further elucidate the electronic structure and coordination configuration of Cu in Bi@SA Cu−C with standard Cu foil, Cu<sub>2</sub>O, and CuO spectra as references. The Cu K-edge XANES of Bi@SA Cu−C is between Cu<sub>2</sub>O and CuO, manifesting the valence state of Cu falls within Cu(I) and Cu(II) (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2b</a>). The fitting curves of the first derivative verifies the valence state of Cu is about +1.5 (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2c</a>). The remarkable peak at 1.52 Å in the Fourier transform (FT) k<sup>3</sup>-weighted EXAFS spectrum in Bi@SA Cu−C is similar to the contribution of Cu<sub>2</sub>O and CuO standard samples, indicating the majority of Cu substances coordinate with C or O to form atomically distributed Cu−C or Cu−O moiety due to the very closed atomic numbers of C and O (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure S8a-d). It is noteworthy that no significant peak at near 2.27 Å is observed, meaning the content of Cu−Cu bond is negligible. By extracting the coordination configuration by quantitative least squares EXAFS fitting, we obtain that the coordination number of Cu is 3 and the average bond length is about 1.97 Å (Table S1). Since the atomic numbers of C and O are very close, and the bond length of M−C or M−O (M represents metal atom) is very similar, it is difficult to distinguish them by the EXAFS characterization.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0041_R_d9436776e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Research">41</a></sup></span> So some theory models were constructed to further determined the coordination configuration of Cu by density functional theory (DFT). For the Cu−O<sub>3</sub> coordination model (Figure S9), it could not stabilize the single Cu atom after structure optimization because the binding energy of Cu−O is greater than zero, as also demonstrated by the report.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0042_R_d9436776e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Phys. Chem. C">42</a></sup></span> In contrast, the coordination of Cu and C via a Cu−C<sub>3</sub> configuration can maintain good structural stability after structural optimization (Figure S10), which proves the Cu−C coordination in this material. Based on the above analysis, we successfully verify that the Cu atom in Bi@SA Cu−C is atomically fixed to the carbon carrier via the Cu−C<sub>3</sub> coordination configurations. The <i>k</i> space fitting curve of EXAFS also exhibits the best fitting degree of Cu−C coordination (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2f</a>, Figure S8e–h). The wavelet transform (WT) is also adopted to analyze the K-edge EXAFS oscillation of Cu. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2g</a>, the position of the strongest peak for Bi@SA Cu−C is obviously different from those of standard Cu foil, Cu<sub>2</sub>O and CuO samples (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">2h</a>–<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0002">j</a>), which further indicates the atomic isolation. The strongest signal at 5.3 Å<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the Cu−C bond in Bi@SA Cu–C, highly consistent with EXAFS results. The Raman spectra of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi @C exist three peaks. The peak at 1584 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to metal Bi, and the peaks located at ≈1325 and 1584 cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to D and G bands of carbon. After forming the Cu single atom, the value of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> increases obviously, indicating the Cu single atoms could induce more defects in the carbon frameworks (Figure S11). The abundant defects are beneficial to providing additional active sites for ion storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0043_R_d9436776e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - P. Natl. Acad. Sci. U. S. A.">43</a></sup></span> By thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) measurement, the contents of Bi and C in Bi@SA Cu−C is 77.09 wt % and 21.50 wt % (Figure S12). The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption were performed to investigate the pore structure of the samples. The obvious hysteresis loop for both samples suggest the existence of mesopores (Figure S13). The specific surface area of Bi@SA Cu−C is 123.4 cm<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, larger than 94.8 cm<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for Bi@C, indicating Cu single atom can generate more pores.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical sodium storage behaviors of the samples were investigated and compared to illustrate the effect of Cu single atom. As displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3a</a>, during the first discharge, the cathodic peak at 0.31 V in cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves is in accordance with the formation of SEI. The two peaks at 0.63 and 0.39 V are associated with the alloying processes from Bi to NaBi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi, respectively. Correspondingly, two anodic peaks at 0.61 and 0.78 V are assigned to the inverse dealloying reactions. Importantly, obvious differences can be identified in the voltage window and integrated area between Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C for the irreversible reduction peaks (Figure S14), which indicates that Cu−C<sub>3</sub> sites can affect the formation of SEI film.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417602-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/45119dc4-93b3-414d-a647-a677d84f1472/anie202417602-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/45119dc4-93b3-414d-a647-a677d84f1472/anie202417602-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/662a9b05-fe8a-4318-93e2-d6b4e9b2e027/anie202417602-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/45119dc4-93b3-414d-a647-a677d84f1472/anie202417602-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417602-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417602" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) CV curves of Bi@SA Cu−C at 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) GCD profiles of Bi@SA Cu−C at 0.2 A g<sup>≅1</sup>. c) Rate capability of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C. d) GCD profiles of Bi@SA Cu−C at different current densities. e,f) Cycling performance and CE of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (e) and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (f). g) The comparison of rate capability for Bi@SA Cu−C with reported Bi-based materials. h) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients. j) in-situ XRD pattern of the Bi@SA Cu−C electrode. k) Schematic illustration of the phase transformation mechanism of Bi during charge/discharge process.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In-situ XRD test was conducted to deeper insight into the sodium storage mechanism of Bi@SA Cu−C by measuring two and half discharge/charge cycles. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3j</a>,<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">k</a>, as the first discharge process proceed, the diffraction peak of Bi at 27.2° and40° faded away and ultimately vanished. Meanwhile, the characteristic peaks of NaBi alloy begin to appear and gradually intense when the voltage dropped to 0.61 V. With the deeper discharge to near 0.41 V, NaBi phase further converted into Na<sub>3</sub>Bi until the end of discharge. In the following charging process, with the deintercalation of sodium ions, the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phase began to partially transform into NaBi near 0.61 V, and the transformation process is complete at 0.79 V. When the voltage further increased, the NaBi phase re-transformed into Bi. The subsequent cycles strictly observed such phase transition, indicating a good reversibility of Bi@SA Cu−C during the Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation/deintercalation. Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3b</a> exhibits galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves of the Bi@SA Cu−C at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The first discharge and charging capacity are 525 and 404 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 77.0 %, much higher than 66.8 % of Bi@C (Figure S15), which is associated with the less decomposition of organic electrolyte to form a thinner SEI film for Bi@SA Cu−C. As current density increase to 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity for Bi@SA Cu−C basically remains unchanged at 403 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at 70 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it still exhibits an impressive specific capacity of 315 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and the specific capacity recovers to 397 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after the current density back to 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, highlighting the excellent rate and reversibility (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3c</a>,<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">d</a>). As expected, the excellent performance of Bi@SA Cu−C locate at the top-level compared with the advanced Bi-based anode (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3g</a>, Table S2). In contrast, the Bi@C sample only shows a specific capacity of 321 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and it drops significantly after the current density exceeds 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, with increasing the amount of Cu doping, the specific capacity of Bi/Cu@C sample decreases (Figure S16). After 1500 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the Bi@SA Cu−C keeps a stable capacity almost without capacity attenuation, showing good structural stability (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3e</a> and Figure S17). Even under 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it can deliver a high reversible specific capacity of 385.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 6000 cycles with high capacity retention rate of 95.8 % and low capacity decay rate of 0.0007 % per cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure S18). While the Bi@C electrode show long-term cycling failure after 4600 cycles owing to micro-short circuits.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0044_R_d9436776e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Joule">44</a></sup></span> At high loading density of ~3.3 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, the Bi@SA Cu−C electrode still delivers a reversible discharge capacity of 394 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, slightly smaller than the values of 404 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at loading density of ~1.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The Bi@SA Cu−C also exhibit good cycle performance with a specific capacity of 356 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S19). Through quantitative analysis of the sodium storage kinetics, the reasons for the remarkable performance of Bi@Cu−C are further revealed. The capacity contribution controlled by diffusion or capacitance process is quantized according to the formula i=av<sup>b</sup>, where i and v are peakcurrent and scanning speed. The b value approaching 0.5 or 1.0 indicates diffusion control dominated process or capacitance control dominated process, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0045_R_d9436776e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0046_R_d9436776e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Mater.">46</a></sup></span> By analyzing the cathode and anode peak, the b values are in the range of 0.50–0.60 (Figure S20), indicating predominant diffusion control characteristic. The ratios of capacitive contribution were calculated, and at the same scan rate, the Bi@SA Cu−C exhibits higher capacitive contribution than Bi@C, in accordance with the high rate capability for the former (Figure S21 and Figure S22). By analyzing the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) curves, the Bi@SA Cu−C electrode clearly shows a much higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0003">3h</a> and Figure S23), proving the introduction of Cu SA can indeed improve the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>As known, SEI film plays a significant effect on cycling life and rate capability of batteries. To explore the origin of how Cu single atom improve the sodium storage performance, we did a detailed characteristic on SEI film from the formation to evolution. Firstly, the microstructure of SEI film was examined by TEM after 1000 cycles. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">c</a> and Figure S24, Bi@SA Cu−C electrode still maintains a uniform morphology with the clearly visible nanospheres and carbon layers, and a uniform and dense SEI film was generated on the surface of Bi@SA Cu−C. The thickness of SEI film for Bi@SA Cu−C is ~6 nm, thinner than that of the Bi@C (~12 nm), which can facilitate faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport dynamics and improve ICE and rate capability. From the HRTEM image, it can be observed that some nanocrystals with discernible lattice fringes of 0.241 nm are distributed in the inner layer of SEI film, which belong to the (200) crystal plane of NaF (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4b</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0047_R_d9436776e930" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span> The composition of SEI film after the first cycle was further analyzed by XPS. As shown in Figure S25a, only one peak is detected for the Na 1s spectra, which is indexed to the Na−O/Na−F. The Na−O/Na−F peak in Bi@SA Cu−C shift toward higher binding energy compared with that of Bi@C, indicating the different contents of Na<sub>2</sub>O/NaF.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0028_R_d9436776e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span> The difference of Na<sub>2</sub>O/NaF content between Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C is distinguished by deconvolving the O 1s and F 1s spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4d</a>, <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">e</a>). The O 1s spectra of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C can be divided into Na−O, C−O/H−O, and Na KLL. There is no visible difference for the relative content of the three peaks in Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C. In F 1s spectrum, the peaks at 683.8 eV is assigned to Na−F bonds, which could be derived from the decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in the electrolyte. It is noteworthy that the relative content of NaF in SEI film for Bi@SA Cu−C (35.5 %) is much higher than that of Bi@C (12.7 %). The high content of inorganic components such as NaF can lower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier and enhance mechanical strength of the SEI film, thus effectively promote the interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> transfer rate and improve cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0048_R_d9436776e955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nat. Commun.">48</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4f</a>, the C1s spectra of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C all can be deconvolved to C−C/C=C, C−O, C=O, and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0049_R_d9436776e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span> Besides, there exists C−Cu bond for Bi@SA Cu−C sample. The first three are associated with sodium-containing organics species, and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> belongs to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, where Bi@SA Cu−C contains more CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> than Bi@C. The obvious difference in Na−F and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> content demonstrates that the Cu single atom can effectively regulate the composition of the SEI film. After first discharge, the Cu 2p spectrum of Bi@SA Cu−C presents a certain distortion compared with the original one (Figure S25b), indicating the Cu single atom plays a catalytic role in the formation of SEI film.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0028_R_d9436776e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span> The interfacial charge transfer dynamics and SEI evolution of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C electrodes were explored by in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4g</a>,<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">h</a> and Table S3). For Bi@SA Cu−C electrode, the impedances are generally consistent at potential &gt;0.5 V, and increases significantly at 0.4 V due to electrolyte decomposition to form SEI film. Then it gradually decreases to a stable level owing to the re-arrangement of SEI components and the formation of conductive film.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417602-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417602-bib-0050_R_d9436776e992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Energy Chem.">50</a></sup></span> Obviously, both the R<sub>SEI</sub> (SEI resistance) and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (charge transfer resistance) values of Bi@SA Cu−C are smaller than those of Bi@C (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4i</a>), which manifest the SEI on the surface of Bi@SA Cu−C is thinner and more robust, in consistent with the TEM image (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">c</a> and Figure S24). Therefore, a thin and uniform SEI layer dominated by NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> can be formed on the surface of Bi@SA Cu−C, which is conductive to the sodium storage performance (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0004">4j</a>).To further illustrate the catalytic mechanism of Cu single atom on the SEI formation, the DFT calculation were performed by constructing structural models of NaPF<sub>6</sub> and 1,2-Dimethoxyethane (DME) on Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> and Bi/defective graphene (Bi/DG) configuration according the microstructural features of Bi@SA Cu−C and Bi@C (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">5</a>, Figure S26 and Figure S27). The results show that the Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> configuration reduces the bond dissociation energy (<i>E</i><sub>d</sub>) of the P−F bond in NaPF<sub>6</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">5a</a>,<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">b</a> and Figure S27a) and the C−O bond in DME on the surface (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">5c</a>,<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">d</a> and Figure S27b) compared with the Bi/DG configuration, demonstrating that the Cu−C<sub>3</sub> configuration can catalyze the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> and DME. Then the electron-rich F react with Na<sup>+</sup> to form NaF-enriched SEI layer, which is consistent with XPS and TEM results. In addition, as an electron donor, single atom Cu can act as ideal sodiophilic site, as confirmed by the charge density difference and the electron localization function (Figure S28). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">5e</a>, the substantial charge transfers from C to its adjacent Cu, with electron accumulation on Cu (cyan regions) and electron depletion on C (yellow regions). The corresponding slice (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">5f</a>) indicates the Cu−C<sub>3</sub> configuration triggers an uneven charge distribution from Cu to neighboring C atoms and generates in-plane local electric field (LEF). The in-plane LEF further acts as a promoter to facilitate the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> and reduce the energy barrier (<i>E</i><sub>m</sub>) for Na<sup>+</sup> migration (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0005">5g</a>, Figure S29 and Figure S30). Specifically, the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) for Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> is 1.07 eV, much higher than 0.21 of Bi/DG. The corresponding <i>E</i><sub>m</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup> is 0.189 eV on the Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> model, much lower than 0.215 eV for Bi/DG, which are consistent with the GITT results.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417602-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f796b3a3-2c1c-4c8b-ab8e-cf62ee34bd6c/anie202417602-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f796b3a3-2c1c-4c8b-ab8e-cf62ee34bd6c/anie202417602-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05e9fdb3-474d-4d21-8cf6-fb2003c52750/anie202417602-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f796b3a3-2c1c-4c8b-ab8e-cf62ee34bd6c/anie202417602-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417602-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8baec877-eb0e-41bf-8adb-86175b2627ca/anie202417602-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8baec877-eb0e-41bf-8adb-86175b2627ca/anie202417602-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/07fe724b-6097-4723-987b-fc3144748dca/anie202417602-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8baec877-eb0e-41bf-8adb-86175b2627ca/anie202417602-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417602-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417602" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a,c) The simulated decomposition process of NaPF<sub>6</sub> (a) and DME (c) on Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> model. b,d) The <i>E</i><sub>d</sub> of P−F bond (b) and C−O bond (d) on Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> and Bi/DG models. e) Top views of charge density difference of Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> model. The cyan and yellow regions represent electron accumulation and depletion, respectively. f) The corresponding slice of charge density difference for Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> model. g) The <i>E</i><sub>m</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup> on Bi/CuC<sub>3</sub> and Bi/DG models.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The excellent performance of Bi@SA Cu−C has inspired us to evaluate the practical application possibility. Then, the full cell was assembled utilizing the commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C as cathode and Bi@SA Cu−C as anode, which is designated as Bi@Cu−C//NVP@C (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0006">6a</a> and Figure S31). To optimize the performance of the full cell, the mass ratio of the anode and cathode was set at 1 : 3. The high coincidence of CV curve and GCD profile verified the good cycle reversibility of the full cell (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0006">6c</a>,<a href="#anie202417602-fig-0006">d</a>). The Bi@Cu−C//NVP@C battery delivers a reversible specific capacity of 351 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a corresponding energy density of 265 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total active mass of anode and cathode), which is superior than the recently reported Bi-based sodium-ion batteries (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0006">6e</a> and Table S4). Moreover, it also shows excellent cycle stability over 400 cycles with 84.8 % capacity retention at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417602-fig-0006">6f</a>). Totally, the prominent performances of the full cell demonstrate the potential for practical application
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417602-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5a8a47ee-362c-4fd0-9a29-2740277dd56f/anie202417602-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5a8a47ee-362c-4fd0-9a29-2740277dd56f/anie202417602-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ca266244-1344-42c5-9480-3b692548abd0/anie202417602-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5a8a47ee-362c-4fd0-9a29-2740277dd56f/anie202417602-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417602-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417602" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">

         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417602-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417602-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a Bi@SA Cu−C microrod with Cu−C<sub>3</sub> single sites was firstly designed and synthesized by a general and convenient pyrolysis of MOF strategy. DFT calculations reveal that the Cu−C<sub>3</sub> configuration can act as a tandem effect to catalyze the formation of stable and inorganic-rich SEI film by reducing the dissociation energy of P−F bond and C−O bond in NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME electrolyte and facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport dynamics by inducing the local electric field. In situ EIS and ex-situ XPS as well as TEM consolidate the reliability result of DFT calculations. The synergistic effects of catalysis and promotion for the Cu−C<sub>3</sub> configurations enhance the specific capacity, rate capability and cycling durability. As the anode of SIBs, the Bi@SA Cu−C presents state-of-the-art sodium storage performance with a specific capacity of 404 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 315 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 70 A g<sup>−1</sup> as well as long cycling stability, much higher than those of Bi@C without Cu single atom. A Na full cell with Bi@SA Cu−C as anode is assembled, which demonstrates a competitive energy density. This work provides a guidance for designing high-performance alloy-based anode by constructing single-atom modulator.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

               <h1></h1>
